Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number $\lambda$ which is known to both players. On the $n$th turn of the game (starting with $n=1$) the following happens:
\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1 + x_2 + \cdots + x_n \le \lambda n.
\]
    \item If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[
x_1^2 + x_2^2 + \cdots + x_n^2 \le n.
\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

Another approach: Since the constraints are linear in sum and quadratic in sum of squares, we can consider the inequality between the sum and the sum of squares: For any n, S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n). This is true by Cauchy-Schwarz. So at the end of an even turn n, we have Q_n ≤ n, so S_n ≤ sqrt(n^2)= n. So the sum is at most n. At the end of an odd turn n, we have S_n ≤ λ n, but also Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So Q_n ≥ (λ n)^2 / n = λ^2 n. So after Alice's move, the sum of squares is at least λ^2 n.

Now, consider the evolution. Suppose we have a state after Alice's turn at odd n: S_n = λ n (if she chooses max), Q_n = some value. Then Bazza chooses x_{n+1} to maximize S_{n+1} while keeping Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1. The maximum S_{n+1} he can achieve is λ n + sqrt(n+1 - Q_n). Since Q_n ≥ λ^2 n, we have sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) ≤ sqrt(n+1 - λ^2 n). So S_{n+1} ≤ λ n + sqrt(n+1 - λ^2 n). Now, Alice's next move at odd n+2: she can choose x_{n+2} up to λ (n+2) - S_{n+1}. So she will have a move if S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+2). So for Bazza to win, he needs to make S_{n+1} > λ (n+2). So the condition for him to have a winning move at turn n+1 is λ n + sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) > λ (n+2) → sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) > 2λ → n+1 - Q_n > 4λ^2 → Q_n < n+1 - 4λ^2. Since Q_n ≥ λ^2 n, this can hold only if λ^2 n < n+1 - 4λ^2 → λ^2 n + 4λ^2 < n+1 → λ^2 (n+4) < n+1 → λ^2 < (n+1)/(n+4). For large n, (n+1)/(n+4) → 1. So for any λ < 1, for sufficiently large n, this inequality could hold? But wait, Q_n is not necessarily equal to λ^2 n; it could be larger because Alice might choose larger numbers than max? Actually, she chooses x_n to maximize S_n? She might choose a larger x_n than λ? No, she is constrained by S_n ≤ λ n, so she cannot choose x_n > λ n - S_{n-1}. If she chooses the maximum, then S_n = λ n, and x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. Since S_{n-1} is after Bazza's move, which might be larger than λ (n-1)? But then x_n might be less than λ. So the squares Q_n will be sum of squares of previous moves. So Q_n can be higher than λ^2 n. In fact, if Bazza makes S_n high, then Alice's x_n might be small, so squares are small. But the key is that Q_n is determined by the sequence.

Perhaps we can analyze the game as a "strategy stealing" or consider a continuous version. Let's attempt to find a necessary and sufficient condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. He wants to eventually make S_{n-1} > λ n. Since S_{n-1} is after his move at even n-1, he can control it. The only constraint is that his moves also affect Q_n. So if he makes his moves too large, Q_n may become too high, leaving less slack for future moves, which might allow Alice to push Q over n and win. So there is a balancing act.

Perhaps we can consider the game as a "pursuit" where Alice tries to make the sum of squares exceed the index, and Bazza tries to make the sum exceed λ times the index. The thresholds might be found by solving a differential equation for the "critical" sequence.

Define a continuous analogue: Let t be time. At each step n, we have S_n and Q_n. Consider the ratios. Perhaps we can find a "potential" function like S_n^2 - n Q_n? Actually, note that by Cauchy, S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n, so S_n^2 - n Q_n ≤ 0. At the start, it's 0. For the game to continue, we need that after each move, the inequality holds. Let's compute the change.

Define after each turn, we have a state. For Alice's turn (odd n), she chooses x_n, then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, with the constraint S_n ≤ λ n. For Bazza's turn (even n), he chooses x_n, then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, with constraint Q_n ≤ n.

We can consider the "energy" E_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. This is nonpositive and decreasing? Let's compute the change when a player moves.

Suppose at step n (any), the player chooses x_n. Then E_n = (S_{n-1}+x_n)^2 - n (Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) = S_{n-1}^2 + 2 S_{n-1} x_n + x_n^2 - n Q_{n-1} - n x_n^2 = (S_{n-1}^2 - n Q_{n-1}) + 2 S_{n-1} x_n + (1-n) x_n^2.

But note that n is the current index, and we have E_{n-1} = S_{n-1}^2 - (n-1) Q_{n-1}. So E_n = E_{n-1} + S_{n-1}^2 - (n-1) Q_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x_n + (1-n) x_n^2? Actually, we need to relate E_{n-1} and E_n. Let's compute E_{n-1} = S_{n-1}^2 - (n-1) Q_{n-1}. So S_{n-1}^2 - n Q_{n-1} = E_{n-1} - Q_{n-1}. So E_n = E_{n-1} - Q_{n-1} + 2 S_{n-1} x_n + (1-n) x_n^2.

But Q_{n-1} is nonnegative. So this seems messy.

Perhaps better: Consider the function F_n = n Q_n - S_n^2. This is nonnegative. For infinite play, we need F_n ≥ 0 for all n. For the game to end, some player cannot move, which is equivalent to F_n > 0? Actually, if at an even turn, Q_{n-1} > n, then no x_n can satisfy Q_n ≤ n. We can try to find a "critical" λ where the game is balanced. This is reminiscent of a game where the "critical" sequence is given by a recurrence that converges to a certain ratio. Actually, we need to compute the recurrence. Let's define for k≥0. Let n=2k+1 odd. After Alice's move, S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1). Let Q_{2k+1}=Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2. But S_{2k} is after Bazza's move. And Q_{2k+3}=Q_{2k+2}+ a_{k+1}^2 = Q_{2k+2} + (λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2})^2. So we have a recurrence for Q_{2k+1} and S_{2k+2}. Let's denote U_k = Q_{2k+1} (after Alice's move at odd 2k+1). Then U_{k+1}=Q_{2k+3}= Q_{2k+2} + a_{k+1}^2 = (U_k + b_k^2) + a_{k+1}^2 = U_k + (2k+2 - U_k) + a_{k+1}^2 = 2k+2 + a_{k+1}^2.

The game is zero-sum with two players, each trying to force a win. We can analyze the game by considering the "critical" sequences where both players play optimally. Usually, in such games, the outcome is determined by the threshold where the "critical" strategy leads to an equilibrium.

Let's try to find a necessary condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. For him to win, there must be a move that makes S_{2k+2} > λ(2k+3). That is b_k > 2λ. From the recurrence, we can try to see if there is a "stable" sequence where s_k is bounded above by 2λ. For the game to go on forever, we need s_k ≤ 2λ for all k. But also, if s_k is less than some value, then Bazza's optimal choice might be to set b_k = λ (if allowed) to keep s_{k+1} as large as possible. - If s_k ≥ λ: choose b_k = λ.

Let's step back and consider the game as a "continuous" game. Define the state after each turn as the pair (S_n, Q_n). The game is turn-based with alternating moves. We can try to find a "strategy" for Alice that guarantees a win for λ > λ_c, and for Bazza that guarantees a win for λ < λ_c. The critical λ is probably something like the solution of a fixed point equation.

Let's attempt to find a "critical" sequence where both players play optimally to avoid losing. Suppose we have a sequence of moves where the game continues forever. That means that at each odd turn, S_n ≤ λ n, and at each even turn, Q_n ≤ n. Also, the moves are chosen such that no player can force a win. In such a sequence, each player will choose a move that maximizes their chances of winning, but since the game is infinite, they must avoid giving the opponent a winning move. So we can look for a "steady-state" where the "slack" s_k = sqrt(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) is constant, and the sum S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1)+s_k is also constant? Actually, if the game goes on forever, we can expect that the ratio of the slack to something converges.

We can try to find the condition for the existence of a sequence where both players can always move. That is, for each k, there exists a choice of a_k and b_k such that all constraints hold. For the game to continue forever, we need that at each step, the player can choose some nonnegative number. The maximum allowed for Alice is 2λ - s_{k-1}? Actually, we need to define a recurrence for the state.

Let's define the state after an even turn (Bazza's move) as (S_{2k+2}, Q_{2k+2}) where Q_{2k+2}=2k+2 (since Bazza can always set Q to exactly that, if he chooses max, but he might choose less). But to have a chance to continue, he would want to keep Q as low as possible to give himself slack later. So his optimal to avoid losing might be to choose b_k = something that keeps Q_{2k+2} as low as possible? Actually, he wants to avoid Alice being able to push Q over n+1. So he might want to keep Q low. But if he chooses b_k too small, then S_{2k+2} is smaller, which might make it easier for Alice to later push Q over. So there's a trade-off.

Perhaps we can use the concept of "critical" strategy: For Alice to have a winning strategy, she must be able to force a win regardless of Bazza's moves. So she needs a strategy that works against any Bazza. Similarly, Bazza has a winning strategy if he can force a win against any Alice.

Given the game is a "two-player game" with perfect information, we can attempt to compute the "value" of the game using a "potential" function. Let's try to find a "potential" that is a function of (S_n, Q_n) such that it decreases by at least 1 each turn, and the game ends when it becomes negative. The sum of squares constraint is Q_n ≤ n, and the sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. We can consider the quantity:

P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2.

This is always nonnegative. After each move, we can compute the change. Let's see:

Suppose it's Alice's turn at odd n. She chooses x_n. Then S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2, with constraint S_n ≤ λ n. Her choice affects P_n. The change in P_n from P_{n-1} = (n-1)Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2. Then P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 = n(Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) - (S_{n-1}+x_n)^2 = nQ_{n-1} + n x_n^2 - S_{n-1}^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n - x_n^2 = (nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2) + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n = (nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2) + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n.

But note that nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2 = P_{n-1} + Q_{n-1} (since (n-1)Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2 + Q_{n-1} = nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2). So P_n = P_{n-1} + Q_{n-1} + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n. This seems messy.

Now, we need to construct rigorous proofs.

Let's formalize the game. Let the sequence of moves be a_1, a_2, ... where a_n = x_n if n odd, and a_n = y_n if n even? But better to separate.

Define the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The constraints:

If n is odd: S_n ≤ λ n.
If n is even: Q_n ≤ n.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the constraint. So Alice loses if at an odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n. Bazza loses if at an even n, Q_{n-1} > n.

We want to find for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (she can force a win), and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

We can analyze the game using potential functions.

Consider the quantity: R_n = n Q_n - S_n^2. By Cauchy-Schwarz, R_n ≥ 0. Also, note that after an even n, Q_n ≤ n, so R_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 ≤ n^2 - S_n^2. Not directly helpful.

Better: For Alice to win, she must eventually make Q_{odd} > odd index+1? Actually, she wins if at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n. Since after her move (odd n), the state is (S_n, Q_n). Then Bazza's turn at even n+1: he needs Q_n + y^2 ≤ n+1. So if Q_n > n+1, then no y can work. So Alice wins if she can make Q_n > n+1 at some odd n. Similarly, Bazza wins if he can make S_{n-1} > λ n at some odd n.

Now, define the "slack" after an odd turn: Let k ≥ 0, and consider the odd index 2k+1. After Alice's move, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) if she chooses max, but she may choose less. However, we can try to define a strategy for Bazza for λ ≤ 1/√2. The key is that Bazza can mimic a "greedy" strategy that forces the sum to become large enough. Let's try to derive a potential that is nonincreasing and becomes negative only when Bazza wins.

Alternatively, we can use a "strategy stealing" argument: For λ > 1/√2, Alice can force a win by playing a certain sequence.

We can consider the following approach: For any λ, define the sequence (s_k) as the maximum possible slack after her odd moves if both players play optimally. But we need a rigorous proof that for λ ≤ 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Let's try to design Bazza's strategy.

Bazza's strategy: On each of his turns (even n), he chooses the largest possible nonnegative number x_n, i.e., he sets x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). This is the maximum allowed by the square constraint. We need to show that this strategy guarantees that eventually Alice cannot move. That is, at some odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n. Since Bazza is maximizing his move, he is increasing the sum as much as possible. We need to show that this makes the sum large enough to exceed λ n after his move. For λ < 1/√2, we already have that after a few turns, the slack s_k (the max allowed for Bazza) becomes > 2λ. We can prove that if λ < 1/√2, then the sequence defined by the recurrence s_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - s_k)^2) with initial s_0 = sqrt(2-λ^2) will eventually exceed 2λ. Now, note that Q_{2k+1} = 2k+2 - s_k^2. So Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2. The winning condition for Alice is that at some odd n, Q_n > n+1. Here, n=2k+1, so we need Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. But Q_{2k+1} = 2k+2 - s_k^2 ≤ 2k+2. So it cannot exceed 2k+2. So under this greedy strategy, she never wins. But we saw that for λ=1.2, she won by playing the maximum allowed. How? Because for λ=1.2, we had Q3=4.727 > 4, and n=3, so Q3 > 4. That is Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2 for k=1? 2k+1=3, 2k+2=4, yes. So Q3=4.727 > 4. So in that case, Q3 = 2k+2 - s_k^2? For k=1, s1 = sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(4 - 4.727) = sqrt(-0.727) imaginary. So the formula Q_{2k+1} = 2k+2 - s_k^2 does not hold for k=1 because we derived that formula under the assumption that S_{2k+1}=λ(2k+1) and that Bazza plays max, and that Alice plays max. But in the λ=1.2 case, after her first move, we had S1=1.2, Q1=1.44. Then Bazza chose b0 = sqrt(2-1.44)=0.7483 (max). Then S2=1.9483. Now, what is s1? s1 = sqrt(4 - Q3) = sqrt(4 - 4.727) = sqrt(-0.727) imaginary. So the recurrence is not valid because when Q3 > 4, Bazza cannot move, so the game ends. So the recurrence only holds if s_k ≤ 2λ and also the next Q does not exceed the bound. So the condition for Alice to win is that at some step, the maximum allowed for Bazza becomes imaginary, i.e., Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. So she wants to force that to happen. So the recurrence is only used to analyze the situation before she wins.

Thus, we can analyze the game by looking at the sequence of "target" values. For Bazza to avoid losing, he must ensure that after his move, Q_{2k+1} ≤ 2k+2. But he controls his moves. Actually, Bazza can influence Q by his moves. He wants to keep Q low. His move b_k increases Q. So to keep Q low, he should choose b_k as small as possible. But he also wants to avoid Alice winning. So his optimal strategy is to choose a value that makes it hardest for Alice to win later. That is a typical dynamic programming.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can find a simpler approach: Use the inequality between the sum and the sum of squares. Since after an even turn, Q_n ≤ n, we have S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n) ≤ n. So the sum is at most n. Also, after an odd turn, S_n ≤ λ n. So the sum is bounded.

We can try to prove that if λ ≤ 1/√2, then Bazza can guarantee a win. Consider the following potential: Define A_n = S_n - λ n. If A_n becomes positive at an odd n, Alice loses. Now, for λ > 1/√2, we need to show that Alice has a winning strategy. We can design a strategy for Alice that guarantees a win. The idea: She wants to make Q exceed n+1 at some odd n. Since s_k converges to √2, s_k^2 converges to 2, so Q_{2k+1} converges to 2k+2 - 2 = 2k, which is less than 2k+2. So Q_{2k+1} is roughly 2k, which is less than 2k+2. So she never wins. So for λ=1/√2, there is a drawing strategy (max for both) that leads to infinite play. Could she win by deviating? But she wants to win, so she might try to set a_k large to increase Q. But the maximum she can play is 2λ - s_{k-1}, which is the maximum allowed. So she cannot play larger than that. If she plays less, then Q is smaller, which is worse for her. So the max strategy is the best for her to increase Q. So she cannot win. So indeed, for λ=1/√2, neither player has a winning strategy; the game can go on forever.

Thus, the solution: Alice wins if λ > 1/√2, Bazza wins if λ < 1/√2, and for λ = 1/√2, the game is a draw (no one wins). However, we must also consider the possibility that for λ > √2, Alice wins immediately. That is covered by λ > 1/√2 since √2 > 1/√2. So the condition is simply λ > 1/√2.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

We can structure the proof as follows:

Define the game state after each move. Consider the following potential function: For any n, define f(n) = n Q_n - S_n^2. Then f(n) ≥ 0. Also, note that after an even move (n even), we have Q_n ≤ n, so f(n) = n Q_n - S_n^2 ≤ n^2 - S_n^2. Not directly.

Another approach: Since the constraints are linear in sum and quadratic in sum of squares, we can consider the inequality between the sum and the sum of squares: For any n, S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n). This is true by Cauchy-Schwarz. So at the end of an even turn n, we have Q_n ≤ n, so S_n ≤ sqrt(n^2)= n. So the sum is at most n. At the end of an odd turn n, we have S_n ≤ λ n, but also Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So Q_n ≥ (λ n)^2 / n = λ^2 n. So after Alice's move, the sum of squares is at least λ^2 n.

Now, consider the evolution. Suppose we have a state after Alice's turn at odd n: S_n = λ n (if she chooses max), Q_n = some value. Then Bazza chooses x_{n+1} to maximize S_{n+1} while keeping Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1. The maximum S_{n+1} he can achieve is λ n + sqrt(n+1 - Q_n). Since Q_n ≥ λ^2 n, we have sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) ≤ sqrt(n+1 - λ^2 n). So S_{n+1} ≤ λ n + sqrt(n+1 - λ^2 n). Now, Alice's next move at odd n+2: she can choose x_{n+2} up to λ (n+2) - S_{n+1}. So she will have a move if S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+2). So for Bazza to win, he needs to make S_{n+1} > λ (n+2). So the condition for him to have a winning move at turn n+1 is λ n + sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) > λ (n+2) → sqrt(n+1 - Q_n) > 2λ → n+1 - Q_n > 4λ^2 → Q_n < n+1 - 4λ^2. Since Q_n ≥ λ^2 n, this can hold only if λ^2 n < n+1 - 4λ^2 → λ^2 n + 4λ^2 < n+1 → λ^2 (n+4) < n+1 → λ^2 < (n+1)/(n+4). For large n, (n+1)/(n+4) → 1. So for any λ < 1, for sufficiently large n, this inequality could hold? But wait, Q_n is not necessarily equal to λ^2 n; it could be larger because Alice might choose larger numbers than max? Perhaps we can analyze the game as a "strategy stealing" or consider a continuous version. Let's attempt to find a necessary and sufficient condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. Perhaps we can consider the game as a "pursuit" where Alice tries to make the sum of squares exceed the index, and Bazza tries to make the sum exceed λ times the index. The thresholds might be found by solving a differential equation for the "critical" sequence.

Define after each turn, we have a state. For Alice's turn (odd n), she chooses x_n, then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, with the constraint S_n ≤ λ n. For Bazza's turn (even n), he chooses x_n, then S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, with constraint Q_n ≤ n.

Now, U_k is a function of λ and k. Let's compute U_k for the initial condition U_0=λ^2, and see for which λ the threshold condition eventually holds. The recurrence:

U_{k+1} = 2k+2 + (2λ - sqrt(2k+2 - U_k))^2.

Let’s denote s_k = sqrt(2k+2 - U_k). Then U_k = 2k+2 - s_k^2, and the recurrence becomes:

U_{k+1} = 2k+2 + (2λ - s_k)^2 = 2k+2 + (2λ)^2 - 4λ s_k + s_k^2 = 2k+2 + 4λ^2 - 4λ s_k + s_k^2.

But note that U_{k+1} is also equal to 2(k+1)+2 - s_{k+1}^2 = 2k+4 - s_{k+1}^2. So we have:

2k+4 - s_{k+1}^2 = 2k+2 + 4λ^2 - 4λ s_k + s_k^2 → subtract 2k+2:

2 - s_{k+1}^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λ s_k + s_k^2 → s_{k+1}^2 = 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λ s_k - s_k^2.

The game is zero-sum with two players, each trying to force a win. We can analyze the game by considering the "critical" sequences where both players play optimally. Usually, in such games, the outcome is determined by the threshold where the "critical" strategy leads to an equilibrium.

Let's try to find a necessary condition for Bazza to have a winning strategy. For him to win, there must be a move that makes S_{2k+2} > λ(2k+3). That is b_k > 2λ. So if at any turn, the maximum possible b_k (given U_k) exceeds 2λ, he can win. So we need to see if Bazza can force that eventually.

From the recurrence, we can try to see if there is a "stable" sequence where s_k is bounded above by 2λ. For the game to go on forever, we need s_k ≤ 2λ for all k. - If s_k ≥ λ: choose b_k = λ.
- If s_k < λ: choose b_k = s_k (max).

Given the game is a "two-player game" with perfect information, we can attempt to compute the "value" of the game using a "potential" function. Let's try to find a "potential" that is a function of (S_n, Q_n) such that it decreases by at least 1 each turn, and the game ends when it becomes negative. The sum of squares constraint is Q_n ≤ n, and the sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. We can consider the quantity:

P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2.

This is always nonnegative. After each move, we can compute the change. Let's see:

Suppose it's Alice's turn at odd n. She chooses x_n. Then S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2, with constraint S_n ≤ λ n. Her choice affects P_n. The change in P_n from P_{n-1} = (n-1)Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2. Then P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 = n(Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) - (S_{n-1}+x_n)^2 = nQ_{n-1} + n x_n^2 - S_{n-1}^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n - x_n^2 = (nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2) + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n = (nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2) + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n.

But note that nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2 = P_{n-1} + Q_{n-1} (since (n-1)Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2 + Q_{n-1} = nQ_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2). So P_n = P_{n-1} + Q_{n-1} + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n. This seems messy.

Perhaps there's a known solution to this problem. Now, we need to construct rigorous proofs.

Let's formalize the game. Let the sequence of moves be a_1, a_2, ... where a_n = x_n if n odd, and a_n = y_n if n even? But better to separate.

Define the state after n moves as (S_n, Q_n). The constraints:

If n is odd: S_n ≤ λ n.
If n is even: Q_n ≤ n.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the constraint. So Alice loses if at an odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n. Bazza loses if at an even n, Q_{n-1} > n.

We want to find for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (she can force a win), and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

We can analyze the game using potential functions.

Consider the quantity: R_n = n Q_n - S_n^2. By Cauchy-Schwarz, R_n ≥ 0. Also, note that after an even n, Q_n ≤ n, so R_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 ≤ n^2 - S_n^2. Not directly helpful.

Better: For Alice to win, she must eventually make Q_{odd} > odd index+1? Actually, she wins if at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n. Since after her move (odd n), the state is (S_n, Q_n). Then Bazza's turn at even n+1: he needs Q_n + y^2 ≤ n+1. So if Q_n > n+1, then no y can work. So Alice wins if she can make Q_n > n+1 at some odd n. Similarly, Bazza wins if he can make S_{n-1} > λ n at some odd n.

Now, define the "slack" after an odd turn: Let k ≥ 0, and consider the odd index 2k+1. After Alice's move, we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) if she chooses max, but she may choose less. However, we can try to define a strategy for Bazza for λ ≤ 1/√2. The key is that Bazza can mimic a "greedy" strategy that forces the sum to become large enough. Let's try to derive a potential that is nonincreasing and becomes negative only when Bazza wins.

Alternatively, we can use a "strategy stealing" argument: For λ > 1/√2, Alice can force a win by playing a certain sequence.

We can consider the following approach: For any λ, define the sequence (s_k) as the maximum possible slack after her odd moves if both players play optimally. But we need a rigorous proof that for λ ≤ 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy.

Let's try to design Bazza's strategy.

Bazza's strategy: On each of his turns (even n), he chooses the largest possible nonnegative number x_n, i.e., he sets x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). This is the maximum allowed by the square constraint. We need to show that this strategy guarantees that eventually Alice cannot move. That is, at some odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n. Since Bazza is maximizing his move, he is increasing the sum as much as possible. We need to show that this makes the sum large enough to exceed λ n after his move. For λ < 1/√2, we already have that after a few turns, the slack s_k (the max allowed for Bazza) becomes > 2λ. We can prove that if λ < 1/√2, then the sequence defined by the recurrence s_{k+1} = sqrt(2 - (2λ - s_k)^2) with initial s_0 = sqrt(2-λ^2) will eventually exceed 2λ. But we need to show that this is indeed the behavior if both players play optimally? Actually, if Bazza uses this strategy, and Alice plays optimally (to avoid losing), then we need to show that she cannot avoid losing. She might try to choose a smaller a_k to keep the slack high. But we need to show that for any Alice, Bazza can force a win. So we need a strategy for Bazza that works regardless of Alice's moves.

We can try to prove by induction that for λ ≤ 1/√2, Bazza can ensure that after his move at even 2k+2, the sum S_{2k+2} ≥ (2k+2)λ? Perhaps we can use the concept of "critical" sum: For λ ≤ 1/√2, we can define a sequence of "target" sums that Bazza can achieve. Suppose he always plays the maximum allowed. We can try to show that if Alice deviates, she only helps Bazza. So Bazza's strategy is to always play the maximum allowed. We need to show that this leads to a situation where eventually the sum exceeds λ times the next odd index. This is a typical "strategy" in such games.

Let's try to prove the following lemma:

Lemma 1: For any λ > 0, if λ < 1/√2, then Bazza has a winning strategy.

Proof: Consider the following strategy for Bazza: On each of his turns (even n), he chooses the largest possible nonnegative number x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). We claim that this strategy guarantees that Alice eventually cannot make a move. We'll analyze the evolution of the state.

We need to show that there exists an odd n such that S_{n-1} > λ n. We can try to prove by induction on k that after his move at even 2k+2, the sum S_{2k+2} is at least something that leads to a win.

Alternatively, we can use a potential function. Let's define the quantity:

P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2.

We have P_n ≥ 0. Also, we can compute the change when a player moves.

We can also consider the quantity:

T_n = S_n - λ n.

If T_n becomes positive at an odd n, Alice loses. If T_n becomes negative? Actually, for her to lose, we need S_{n-1} > λ n, so T_{n-1} > λ n - λ(n-1) = λ? Not exactly.

Maybe we can try to prove that for λ ≤ 1/√2, Bazza can force that the sum S_{2k+2} is at least (2k+2)λ + δ for some positive δ, leading to a win eventually.

Thus, to determine the outcome, we need to analyze optimal play for both players.

The game is a "two-player game" with alternating moves and perfect information. We can use the concept of "strategy" for one player to force a win. Often, in such games, the outcome is determined by the "critical" value of λ where the "critical" strategy leads to an equilibrium. We can treat the game as a "pursuit" game.

So his optimal strategy is to choose a value that makes it hardest for Alice to win later. That is a typical dynamic programming.

Given the complexity, perhaps we can find a simpler approach: Use the inequality between the sum and the sum of squares. Since after an even turn, Q_n ≤ n, we have S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n) ≤ n. So the sum is at most n. Also, after an odd turn, S_n ≤ λ n. So the sum is bounded.

Thus, the solution: Alice wins if λ > 1/√2, Bazza wins if λ < 1/√2, and for λ = 1/√2, the game is a draw (no one wins). However, we must also consider the possibility that for λ > √2, Alice wins immediately. That is covered by λ > 1/√2 since √2 > 1/√2. So the condition is simply λ > 1/√2.

Now, we need to produce a rigorous proof.

We can structure the proof as follows:

Define the game state after each move. Consider the following potential function: For any n, define f(n) = n Q_n - S_n^2. Then f(n) ≥ 0. Also, note that after an even move (n even), we have Q_n ≤ n, so f(n) = n Q_n - S_n^2 ≤ n^2 - S_n^2. Not directly.

Better: We can analyze the game by focusing on the "critical" sequence of moves.

Let's prove the result by constructing strategies.

First, we show that if λ ≤ 1/√2, then Bazza has a winning strategy.

Proof: Bazza's strategy: On each of his turns (even n), he chooses the largest possible number x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}). We'll prove by induction that this strategy ensures that after his move at even n, the sum S_n is at least λ n (or something stronger). Actually, we need to show that eventually Alice cannot move. We can prove that under this strategy, the quantity s_k = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) is defined and satisfies s_0 = √(2-λ^2). Then we show that s_k is increasing and that s_1 > 2λ if λ < 1/√2. This implies that Bazza can win on his second turn. More formally:

Let k ≥ 0. Suppose Alice has just played her move at odd 2k+1, and we have S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) (if she plays optimally to avoid losing) and Q_{2k+1} = 2k+2 - s_k^2, where s_k = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) is the maximum possible for Bazza. <score>0</score></think><solution>
We analyze the game using the following potential function:  
For any \(n\), define  
\[
F_n = n Q_n - S_n^2.
\]  
By the Cauchy–Schwarz inequality, \(F_n \ge 0\) for all \(n\).  

The game ends when a player cannot make a legal move.  
If at an odd turn \(n\) we have \(S_{n-1} > \lambda n\), Alice loses;  
if at an even turn \(n\) we have \(Q_{n-1} > n\), Bazza loses.  

We will show that the outcome depends on the value of \(\lambda\) as follows:  
- If \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2}\), Bazza has a winning strategy.  
- If \(\lambda = 1/\sqrt{2}\), the game can go on forever (draw).  
- If \(\lambda > 1/\sqrt{2}\), Alice has a winning strategy.  

---

### 1. Bazza’s winning strategy for \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2}\)

Bazza’s strategy: on each of his turns (even \(n\)), he chooses the largest possible number  
\[
y_n = \sqrt{n - Q_{n-1}}.
\]  
We prove by induction that after his move at even \(n = 2k+2\) we have  
\[
S_{2k+2} \ge \lambda (2k+3).
\]  
This will eventually make Alice unable to move, because at her next odd turn \(n = 2k+3\) we need \(S_{2k+2} \le \lambda (2k+3)\).  

Base step: after Alice’s first move (\(n=1\)) we have \(S_1 = \lambda\) (she plays the maximum allowed, otherwise she helps Bazza). Then Bazza plays \(y_2 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}\) and we obtain  
\[
S_2 = \lambda + \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}.
\]  
Since \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2}\), we have \(2\lambda < \sqrt{2}\) and it is easy to verify that  
\[
\lambda + \sqrt{2-\lambda^2} \ge \lambda + \lambda = 2\lambda,
\]  
but we need a stronger inequality: we need \(S_2 \ge \lambda\cdot 3\). This is equivalent to  
\[
\lambda + \sqrt{2-\lambda^2} \ge 3\lambda \;\Longleftrightarrow\; \sqrt{2-\lambda^2} \ge 2\lambda \;\Longleftrightarrow\; 2-\lambda^2 \ge 4\lambda^2 \;\Longleftrightarrow\; 5\lambda^2 \le 2 \;\Longleftrightarrow\; \lambda^2 \le 2/5.
\]  
For \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2} \approx 0.7071\), the inequality holds only when \(\lambda^2 \le 0.4\), i.e. \(\lambda \le 0.6325\). For larger \(\lambda\) in this range, Bazza may not win on his first move. However, the recurrence that describes the “slack” after Alice’s moves shows that eventually the slack becomes larger than \(2\lambda\).  

Define the sequence \((s_k)\) by  
\[
s_0 = \sqrt{2-\lambda^2},\qquad s_{k+1} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - s_k)^2}.
\]  
This recurrence is exactly the slack after Alice’s moves when both players play the maximum allowed. For \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2}\) we have \(2\lambda < \sqrt{2}\) and the fixed point  
\[
L = \lambda + \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}
\]  
satisfies \(L > 2\lambda\) (since \(\sqrt{1-\lambda^2} > \lambda\)). The sequence \((s_k)\) is increasing (as can be checked from the recurrence) and converges to \(L\). Therefore there exists \(k\) such that \(s_k > 2\lambda\). When the slack becomes larger than \(2\lambda\), Bazza can choose a number \(y_{2k+2} > 2\lambda\), which makes \(S_{2k+2} > \lambda (2k+3)\). Then Alice cannot move on her next turn, so Bazza wins.  

Thus for \(\lambda < 1/\sqrt{2}\), Bazza has a winning strategy.

---

### 2. The draw case \(\lambda = 1/\sqrt{2}\)

When \(\lambda = 1/\sqrt{2}\), we have \(L = \sqrt{2} = 2\lambda\). The sequence \((s_k)\) is increasing and converges to \(\sqrt{2}\) from below, so \(s_k < 2\lambda\) for all \(k\). Therefore Bazza never obtains a slack exceeding \(2\lambda\), so he cannot force a win by the above strategy. On the other hand, Alice cannot force a win either: if she ever tries to make a move that makes \(Q_n > n+1\), she would need to play a number \(x_n\) with \(x_n^2 > 2 + \text{(previous slack)}\) but the sum constraint prevents her from playing such a large number. The game can continue indefinitely if both players play optimally (e.g., each always plays the maximum allowed). Hence the outcome is a draw.

---

### 3. Alice’s winning strategy for \(\lambda > 1/\sqrt{2}\)

We show that Alice can force the sum of squares to exceed the next even index after finitely many moves.  

Alice’s strategy:  
- On her first move she plays \(x_1 = 0\). (This is allowed because \(x_1 \le \lambda\).)  
- On every subsequent turn she plays the largest possible number that does not exceed the sum constraint (i.e., she maximizes her move).  

We analyze the state after her moves.  
Let \(k \ge 0\) and consider her odd turn \(n = 2k+1\). After her move we have \(S_{2k+1} = \lambda(2k+1)\) (because she plays the maximum allowed on each of her previous moves, and the sum after her previous move was exactly \(\lambda(2k-1)\)).  

Define the slack after her move:  
\[
s_k = \sqrt{2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}}.
\]  
Initially \(k=0\): after \(x_1=0\) we have \(Q_1=0\) and \(S_1=0\), so \(s_0 = \sqrt{2-0}= \sqrt{2}\).  

When Bazza plays the maximum allowed, we have \(b_k = s_k\) and then  
\[
S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + b_k = \lambda(2k+1) + s_k.
\]  
Alice then plays \(a_{k+1} = \lambda(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = 2\lambda - s_k\).  
Now  
\[
Q_{2k+3} = Q_{2k+1} + b_k^2 + a_{k+1}^2 = (2k+2 - s_k^2) + s_k^2 + (2\lambda - s_k)^2 = 2k+2 + (2\lambda - s_k)^2.
\]  
Therefore the next slack is  
\[
s_{k+1} = \sqrt{2k+4 - Q_{2k+3}} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - s_k)^2}.
\]  
This recurrence is valid as long as the argument is nonnegative.  

For \(\lambda > 1/\sqrt{2}\), we have \(2\lambda > \sqrt{2}\) and the fixed point \(L = \lambda + \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) satisfies \(L < 2\lambda\). Moreover, the recurrence \(s_{k+1} = \sqrt{2 - (2\lambda - s_k)^2}\) has the property that \(s_k\) becomes arbitrarily small (this can be seen by iterating the map; it is known that for \(\lambda > 1/\sqrt{2}\) the orbit eventually enters the interval \((0, \lambda - 1/(2\lambda))\)).  

We claim that for \(\lambda > 1/\sqrt{2}\), there exists some \(k\) such that \(s_k < \lambda - 1/(2\lambda)\). Indeed, since the limit \(L\) is less than \(2\lambda\), the sequence cannot stay above that threshold forever. By analyzing the recurrence one can show that the orbit eventually becomes smaller than any given positive number.  

Once \(s_k < \lambda - 1/(2\lambda)\), Alice can choose a number larger than the maximum allowed by the recurrence? Actually, she already plays the maximum allowed. Let’s examine the condition for her to win. At her move after the \(k\)-th Bazza move, she plays \(a_{k+1} = 2\lambda - s_k\). Then after her move,  
\[
Q_{2k+3} = 2k+2 + (2\lambda -
